</p><p>Sometime between 633 BCE and 530 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Hanno_the_Navigator" title="Hanno the Navigator">Hanno the Navigator</a> either established or reinforced Phoenician colonies in Western Sahara, but all ancient remains have vanished with virtually no trace.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greeks">Greeks</span></h3>
<p>By 500 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> arrived in the desert. Greek traders spread along the eastern coast of the desert, establishing trading colonies along the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginians</a> explored the Atlantic coast of the desert, but the turbulence of the waters and the lack of markets caused a lack of presence further south than modern Morocco. Centralized states thus surrounded the desert on the north and east; it remained outside the control of these states. Raids from the nomadic Berber people of the desert were of constant concern to those living on the edge of the desert.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_civilization">Urban civilization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ghardaia01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2196" data-file-width="3375" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ghardaia01.jpg/220px-Ghardaia01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ghardaia01.jpg/330px-Ghardaia01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ghardaia01.jpg/440px-Ghardaia01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ghardaia01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Market on the main square of <a href="/wiki/Gharda%C3%AFa" title="Ghardaïa">Ghardaïa</a> (1971)</div></div></div>
<p>An urban civilization, the <a href="/wiki/Garamantes" title="Garamantes">Garamantes</a>, arose around 500 BCE in the heart of the Sahara, in a valley that is now called the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wadi_al-Ajal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wadi al-Ajal (page does not exist)">Wadi al-Ajal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fezzan" title="Fezzan">Fezzan</a>, Libya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakes_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lakes-25">[25]</a></sup> The Garamantes achieved this development by digging tunnels far into the mountains flanking the valley to tap <a href="/wiki/Fossil_water" title="Fossil water">fossil water</a> and bring it to their fields. The Garamantes grew populous and strong, conquering their neighbors and capturing many slaves (who were put to work extending the tunnels). The ancient Greeks and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> knew of the Garamantes and regarded them as uncivilized nomads. However, they traded with them, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_bath" title="Roman bath">Roman bath</a> has been found in the Garamantes' capital of Garama. <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeologists</a> have found eight major towns and many other important settlements in the Garamantes' territory. The Garamantes' civilization eventually collapsed after they had depleted available water in the <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a> and could no longer sustain the effort to extend the tunnels further into the mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keys_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keys-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Berbers">Berbers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG/220px-Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG/330px-Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG/440px-Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zawiya_(institution)" title="Zawiya (institution)">Zawiya</a> at the entrance of <a href="/wiki/Taghit" title="Taghit">Taghit</a>, Algeria</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_caravane2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1390" data-file-width="1040" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hoggar_caravane2.jpg/170px-Hoggar_caravane2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hoggar_caravane2.jpg/255px-Hoggar_caravane2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hoggar_caravane2.jpg/340px-Hoggar_caravane2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_caravane2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> once controlled the central Sahara and its trade.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber people</a> occupied (and still occupy with Arabs) much of the Sahara. The Garamantes Berbers built a prosperous empire in the heart of the desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The Tuareg nomads continue to inhabit and move across wide Sahara surfaces to the present day.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_and_Arabic_expansion">Islamic and Arabic expansion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Trans-Saharan trade</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamization_of_Sudan" title="Islamization of Sudan">Islamization of Sudan</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> ruled the northern shores of the Sahara from the 5th to the 7th centuries. After the Muslim conquest of Arabia, specifically the Arabian peninsula, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_North_Africa" title="Muslim conquest of North Africa">Muslim conquest of North Africa</a> began in the mid-7th to early 8th centuries and Islamic influence expanded rapidly on the Sahara. By the end of 641 all of Egypt was in Muslim hands. Trade across the desert intensified, and a significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> crossed the desert. It has been estimated that from the 10th to 19th centuries some 6,000 to 7,000 slaves were transported north each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Turkish_era">Ottoman Turkish era</span></h3>
<p>In the 16th century the northern fringe of the Sahara, such as coastal regencies in present-day <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Algeria" title="Ottoman Algeria">Algeria</a> and Tunisia, as well as some parts of present-day Libya, together with the semi-autonomous kingdom of Egypt, were occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. From 1517 Egypt was a valued part of the Ottoman Empire, ownership of which provided the Ottomans with control over the Nile Valley, the east Mediterranean and North Africa. The benefit of the Ottoman Empire was the freedom of movement for citizens and goods. Traders exploited the Ottoman land routes to handle the spices, gold and silk from the East, manufactured goods from Europe, and the slave and gold traffic from Africa. Arabic continued as the local language and Islamic culture was much reinforced. The Sahel and southern Sahara regions were home to several independent states or to roaming Tuareg clans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_colonialism">European colonialism</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/European_colonialism" title="European colonialism">European colonialism</a> in the Sahara began in the 19th century. <a href="/wiki/July_Monarchy" title="July Monarchy">France</a> conquered the regency of Algiers from the Ottomans in 1830, and French rule spread south from Algeria and eastwards from <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> into the upper <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger</a> to include present-day Algeria, Chad, Mali then <a href="/wiki/French_Sudan" title="French Sudan">French Sudan</a> including Timbuktu, Mauritania, Morocco (1912), Niger, and Tunisia (1881). By the beginning of the 20th century, the trans-Saharan trade had clearly declined because goods were moved through more modern and efficient means, such as airplanes, rather than across the desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The French Colonial Empire was the dominant presence in the Sahara. It established regular air links from <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a> (HQ of famed <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9ropostale_(aviation)" title="Aéropostale (aviation)">Aéropostale</a>), to <a href="/wiki/Oran" title="Oran">Oran</a> and over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoggar" title="Hoggar">Hoggar</a> to Timbuktu and West to <a href="/wiki/Bamako" title="Bamako">Bamako</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">Dakar</a>, as well as trans-Sahara bus services run by La Companie Transsaharienne (est. 1927).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wauthier1_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-wauthier1-78">[78]</a></sup> A remarkable film shot by famous aviator Captain <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Wauthier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="René Wauthier (page does not exist)">René Wauthier</a> documents the first crossing by a large truck convoy from Algiers to Tchad, across the Sahara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reconnaissance_saharienne_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reconnaissance_saharienne-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egypt, under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali</a> and his successors, conquered Nubia in 1820–22, founded <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> in 1823, and conquered <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> in 1874. Egypt, including the Sudan, became a British protectorate in 1882. Egypt and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> lost control of the Sudan from 1882 to 1898 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">Mahdist War</a>. After its capture by British troops in 1898, the Sudan became an Anglo-Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Condominium_(international_law)" title="Condominium (international law)">condominium</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Spain)" title="Restoration (Spain)">Spain</a> captured present-day <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> after 1874, although Rio del Oro remained largely under <a href="/wiki/Sahrawi_people" title="Sahrawi people">Sahrawi</a> influence. In 1912, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italy</a> captured parts of what was to be named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Libya" title="Ottoman Libya">Libya</a> from the Ottomans. To promote the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> religion in the desert, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">Pope Pius IX</a> appointed a delegate Apostolic of the Sahara and the Sudan in 1868; later in the 19th century his jurisdiction was reorganized into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vicariate_Apostolic_of_Sahara" title="Vicariate Apostolic of Sahara">Vicariate Apostolic of Sahara</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakup_of_the_empires_and_afterwards">Breakup of the empires and afterwards</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/220px-Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/330px-Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/440px-Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A natural rock arch in south western Libya</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_Desert_(Algerie).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg/220px-Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg/330px-Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_Desert_(Algerie).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sahara today</div></div></div>
<p>Egypt became independent of Britain in 1936, although the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_treaty_of_1936" title="Anglo-Egyptian treaty of 1936">Anglo-Egyptian treaty of 1936</a> allowed Britain to keep troops in Egypt and to maintain the British-Egyptian condominium in the Sudan. British military forces were withdrawn in 1954.
</p><p>Most of the Saharan states achieved independence after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>: Libya in 1951; Morocco, Sudan, and Tunisia in 1956; Chad, Mali, Mauritania, and Niger in 1960; and Algeria in 1962. Spain withdrew from Western Sahara in 1975, and it was partitioned between Mauritania and Morocco. Mauritania withdrew in 1979; Morocco continues to hold the territory.
</p><p>In the post–World War II era, several <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a> and communities have developed to utilize the desert's natural resources. These include large deposits of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> in Algeria and Libya, and large deposits of <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a> in Morocco and Western Sahara.
</p><p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Trans-African_Highway_network" title="Trans-African Highway network">Trans-African highways</a> have been proposed across the Sahara, including the <a href="/wiki/Cairo%E2%80%93Dakar_Highway" title="Cairo–Dakar Highway">Cairo–Dakar Highway</a> along the Atlantic coast, the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Sahara_Highway" title="Trans-Sahara Highway">Trans-Sahara Highway</a> from <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> on the Mediterranean to <a href="/wiki/Kano" title="Kano">Kano</a> in Nigeria, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tripoli_%E2%80%93_Cape_Town_Highway" title="Tripoli – Cape Town Highway">Tripoli – Cape Town Highway</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a> in Libya to <a href="/wiki/N%27Djamena" title="N'Djamena">N'Djamena</a> in Chad, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cairo_%E2%80%93_Cape_Town_Highway" title="Cairo – Cape Town Highway">Cairo – Cape Town Highway</a> which follows the Nile. Each of these highways is partially complete, with significant gaps and unpaved sections.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="People_and_languages">People and languages</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabslavers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="556" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arabslavers.jpg/220px-Arabslavers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arabslavers.jpg/330px-Arabslavers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arabslavers.jpg/440px-Arabslavers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabslavers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 19th-century engraving of an Arab <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">slave-trading</a> caravan transporting black African slaves across the Sahara</div></div></div>
<p>The people of the Sahara are of various origins. Among them the Amaziɣ including the Turūq, various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabized" title="Arabized">Arabized</a> Amaziɣ groups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hassaniya" title="Hassaniya">Hassaniya</a>-speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahrawis" title="Sahrawis">Sahrawis</a>, whose populations include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Znaga" title="Znaga">Znaga</a>, a tribe whose name is a remnant of the pre-historic <a href="/wiki/Zenaga_language" title="Zenaga language">Zenaga language</a>. Other major groups of people include the: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toubou" title="Toubou">Toubou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">Nubians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_people" title="Zaghawa people">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songhai_people" title="Songhai people">Songhai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beja_people" title="Beja people">Beja</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula/Fulani</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Peul</i>; <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fula</a>: <i lang="ff-Latn">Fulɓe</i>).
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_dialects" title="Arabic dialects">Arabic dialects</a> are the most widely spoken languages in the Sahara. Arabic, Berber and its variants now regrouped under the term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazigh" title="Amazigh">Amazigh</a> (which includes the <a href="/wiki/Guanche_language" title="Guanche language">Guanche language</a> spoken by the original Berber inhabitants of the Canary Islands) and Beja languages are part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Asiatic" title="Afro-Asiatic">Afro-Asiatic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamito-Semitic" title="Hamito-Semitic">Hamito-Semitic</a> family.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Unlike neighboring <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> and the central governments of the states that comprise the Sahara, the French language bears little relevance to inter-personal discourse and commerce within the region, its people retaining staunch ethnic and political affiliations with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuareg" title="Tuareg">Tuareg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> leaders and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The legacy of the French <a href="/wiki/French_North_Africa" title="French North Africa">colonial era</a> administration is primarily manifested in the territorial reorganization enacted by the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Fourth_Republic" title="French Fourth Republic">Fourth</a> republics, which engendered artificial political divisions within a hitherto isolated and porous region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Diplomacy with local clients was conducted primarily in Arabic, which was the traditional language of bureaucratic affairs. Mediation of disputes and inter-agency communication was served by interpreters contracted by the French government, who, according to Keenan, "documented a space of intercultural mediation," contributing much to preserving the indigenous cultural identities in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-PA1327-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-PA1327_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-PA1327_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://worldwildlife.org/ecoregions/pa1327" rel="nofollow">"Sahara desert"</a>. <i>Terrestrial Ecoregions</i>.
